China Steps In as Trump’s Ceasefire UNRAVELS | China Decode

TL;DR The fragile Middle East ceasefire has collapsed into a U.S.-led blockade of the Strait of Hormuz, placing China in a precarious position: Beijing is facing 50% tariff threats from the Trump administration over alleged military aid to Iran while simultaneously grappling...

TL;DR

The fragile Middle East ceasefire has collapsed into a U.S.-led blockade of the Strait of Hormuz, placing China in a precarious position: Beijing is facing 50% tariff threats from the Trump administration over alleged military aid to Iran while simultaneously grappling with massive energy supply chain disruptions.

The Strait of Hormuz Blockade and Global Escalation

  • U.S.-Led Blockade: On Monday, the U.S. began a formal blockade of the Strait of Hormuz following the collapse of negotiations between Iran and America in Pakistan.
      • Market Shock: Brent oil futures have surged 41% since the conflict began; daily ship traffic through the Strait has decreased by over 90 vessels.
      • Iranian Warning: Tehran has signaled that if its ports are targeted, no shipping in the region will be safe.
  • China’s Economic Exposure: China receives 37.7% of all oil exports transiting the Strait—the most of any single nation.
      • While this represents only 6% of China’s total energy usage, it is a significant disruptor for petrochemical and fertilizer supply chains.
  • Allegations of Military Aid: U.S. intelligence reports suggest China may be preparing to deliver air defense systems to Iran.
      • Dual-Use Tech: The U.S. Defense Intelligence Agency reports that the IRGC is using AI-enhanced satellite imagery from a Chinese firm, MizarVision, to track U.S. operations.

U.S.-China Relations and the "Tariff Trigger"

  • Trump’s Trade Ultimatum: President Trump has threatened 50% tariffs on Chinese goods, with no exemptions, if Beijing is found to be supplying Iran with military weaponry.
      • Diplomatic Fallout: These threats likely "scupper" any planned May summit in Beijing, as the relationship shifts from potential cooperation back to high-stakes economic warfare.
  • The Mediator Role: Despite the tension, Alice Han and James Kynge suggest China may still attempt to pivot back to a peacemaker role via influence in Pakistan, seeking to reopen the Strait to protect its own domestic economy.

The Taiwan Strategic Outlook

  • KMT-Beijing Outreach: Eyck Freymann (Hoover Fellow) highlights the recent meeting between Kuomintang (KMT) leader Chiang Wan-an and Xi Jinping as a Chinese effort to drive a wedge between the U.S. and Taiwan ahead of the 2028 elections.
  • Deterring Crisis vs. War: Freymann argues that the U.S. must focus on deterring a "crisis" (like a blockade or gray-zone pressure) rather than just an amphibious invasion.
      • Military Balance: While China has more ships and missiles, the U.S. maintains qualitative advantages in combined-arms experience, submarine warfare, and space capabilities.
  • The TSMC "Lehman Moment": A kinetic conflict would immediately take TSMC’s semiconductor fabrication off the board.
      • AI Impact: TSMC produces 99% of the advanced Nvidia GPUs used for AI; a conflict would trigger a global recession and the immediate collapse of the U.S. tech trade.

Economic Forecasts and Market Shifts

  • Japanese Auto Crisis: James Kynge predicts a "major disruptive shock" to a Japanese automaker this year (share price collapse or fire-sale merger).
      • Honda’s Struggle: CEO Toshihiro Mibe recently stated the Japanese industry is "on the brink of survival" against Chinese EV cost and speed advantages.
  • China’s Internal Inflation: Alice Han predicts that prolonged Middle East tensions will cause "cost-push inflation" in China.
      • Domestic Impact: Rising input costs for energy and chemicals will eat into Chinese firm profitability and stall Beijing’s efforts to boost domestic consumption.
      • Export Bans: Beijing has already banned exports of diesel, jet fuel, and some fertilizers to protect domestic supply; Han expects this list to expand to plastics and sulfuric acid if the blockade persists.

Notable Quotes

"China gets 37.7% of all of the oil exports that transit through the Strait of Hormuz... China has more to lose from this situation in terms of imported oil than any other country." — James Kynge, China Decode
"If things get hot between the US and China, neither side is going to let TSMC's facilities fall into the hands of the other. And that by itself throws the world economy into recession... bye-bye AI." — Eyck Freymann, Hoover Fellow
"We have no chance against this." — Toshihiro Mibe, CEO of Honda (referring to Chinese EV manufacturing efficiency)
"China geopolitically may be benefiting from some of these disturbances... but domestically and economically, we're starting to see signs that it is suffering from this prolonged blockade." — Alice Han, China Decode

New customers only. Thank you to Huel for partnering and supporting our show. Welcome back. Today we're joined by Eyck Freymann. He is a Hoover Fellow at Stanford University and a research fellow at the US Naval War College's China Maritime Studies Institute. He's also written a new book called Defending Taiwan: A Strategy to Prevent War with China. Eyck, thanks so much for joining us today and welcome to China Decode. Thanks Alice for having me. Eyck, terrific to have you on. Let me just kick off with I think what is a today angle on this story. Chiang Wan-an, the leader of Taiwan's main opposition party, the Kuomintang or the KMT, just met with China's leader Xi Jinping on Friday. And therefore she became the first KMT leader to visit the mainland in over a decade. I'm just wondering what you make of this. Is this just a sideshow or is it a sign of some interesting movement perhaps between mainland China and Taiwan? Well, the first thing to understand is that Chiang is a controversial figure within Taiwan. And she hasn't fully consolidated her control over her own party. Within the KMT she's seen as relatively pro-Beijing, or at least soft on Beijing. And part of the point of meeting with Xi Jinping is to show, "No, I'm the boss. Even he says so." Of course, the price of the meeting was she had to make some comments harshly criticizing the idea of Taiwan independence, essentially blaming the current president Lai Ching-te for the tensions. So I think if I'm Xi Jinping, I see this as a prime opportunity to drive a wedge between the US and Taiwan, and to encourage a more friendly KMT going into the 2028 elections. Great. Well, Eyck, I wanted to take us back into history. And, and for listeners out there, you may or may not know this, Eyck and I were in the same history program at Harvard. So, this is definitely something that we were looking into when we were back there in our undergraduate degree. But can you walk us through the 1949 period onwards, why we ended up in the situation of Two Chinas and frame within that what Xi Jinping's personal goals are when it comes to a so-called reunification. How do you think he is thinking about this in historical terms? In 60 seconds or less. In 60 seconds or less. Yes. I'll... I'll do what I can. So look, Taiwan is the unfinished business of the Chinese Civil War. The Chinese Communist Party and the Kuomintang, Mao Zedong and Chiang Kai-shek, they fought for control of the mainland. Chiang Kai-shek's forces eventually lost control of Northern China and over the course of 1949 they withdrew to Taiwan, taking many of the treasures of the imperial palace with them. They had been the ruling government of China preceding 1949. So they brought with them an unbroken chain of government extending back to the Republic of China constitution, which had been signed in Nanjing. And that is the reason why the Kuomintang today claims that it's the unbroken, sole legitimate representative of a China that happens to have been divided since 1949. Now the issue is the KMT ruled as an autocratic regime for several decades, but in the 90s Taiwan turned into a democracy. And now there's another party that has been in charge for the past decade, the DPP, which has a very different view of history. In their view, Taiwan is and always has been an independent country called Taiwan. That it just happens for some legacy historical reasons to be called the Republic of China. So this is the fundamental problem. The question of Taiwan's status is not just about Taiwan. It's about the definition of what China means. Is there such a thing as One China that involves Taiwan, or is there One China that doesn't include Taiwan, or is there One China but different interpretations of what that One China means? Much of what Taiwan's domestic politics are about is really this core question. And from Beijing's point of view, it's willing to negotiate with anyone on Taiwan who acknowledges that there is only One China and Taiwan is part of China. And this is why not just Xi Jinping but his predecessors have always been open to dialogue with the KMT. But Xi Jinping is particularly interested in meeting KMT figures. He met former Taiwan KMT president Ma Ying-jeou in Singapore in 2015. And I think if the KMT wins the next presidential election in 2028, he would be very happy to return back to the negotiating table. But with much more leverage than he had last time. What would that look like if we were to see the KMT win in 2028? How do you think Beijing is going to play that? So, let's go back to 1949. After Mao takes over the mainland, the dust settles, he's in charge. In 1949, the first thing he does is instruct his generals to prepare an invasion plan to take over Taiwan. This is the unfinished business of the war. And they do the analysis, and they conclude, sir, it can't be done. Not even close. The Taiwan Strait is one of the most difficult waterways in the world. The weather is unpredictable, the meteorological, tidal conditions are very difficult. Taiwan is this mountain fortress. There's only a small number of beaches you can land on. And there's an attempt to squeeze Taiwan out of some of its offshore islands, which might be easier to seize. But then there's two Taiwan Strait Crises with the United States where Washington says, not on our watch. So, Beijing essentially has been denied this prize, and they have been building over time the military capability to increase their chances of pulling off an amphibious invasion. But in my analysis at least, they are still not at the point where they could have confidence that that will work, despite all of the decisions the United States has made to spend down stockpiles of critical munitions in the Middle East. So, that is why diplomacy remains Xi Jinping's preferred vector for achieving what he calls reunification. But of course he doesn't care about whether it's democratically acceptable to the people of Taiwan. He wants a very coerced form of diplomacy. One in which Taiwan feels squeezed on all sides and therefore has no choice. And what he's willing to offer Taiwan is probably something similar to what he offered Hong Kong: One Country, Two Systems, Taiwan plan. And basically it means Taiwan has to capitulate on the principle. It has to admit, yes, there is only one China in the world, we're part of it, and Beijing is the legitimate government. The offer on the table is, if Taiwan will accept some version of that principle, it can maintain self-government, it can maintain its own control over its economic affairs, maybe it can get subsidized energy, other perks. It can enjoy, essentially, some of the benefits of being part of China without the drawbacks of being subject to authoritarian communist control. The reason this has gotten to be a harder sell is that after 2019, when the people of Taiwan watched what happened to Hong Kong, One Country, Two Systems is clearly a weasel word. It means as soon as you step out of line, Beijing will seize the opportunity to bring the hammer down. So that's the reason Taiwan is only ever going to accept some kind of a deal along these lines if it's truly under duress, it has nowhere else to turn. It's so useful to get the proper historical perspective and, and how all of the, the politics of the moment fit together around this issue. But as a China watcher, you know, whenever, pretty much whenever we attend a public event, the first question or one of the questions we always get asked is: Do you think China will invade Taiwan? You've just mentioned what you think Xi Jinping's diplomatic priorities are with regard to Taiwan, but let me just draw you if I can into that kind of invasion scenario. We hear about a quarantine strategy or a quarantine scenario that the mainland could undertake with regard to Taiwan. Could you just clear up what you think of that whole nexus of really sort of incendiary questions lead to? Do you think that Xi Jinping could invade Taiwan? Well, let's break down these scenarios one by one. Xi Jinping wants to build a full menu of options. Options that he can take either simultaneously or in sequence. So one of them is obviously an amphibious invasion. That's cross the strait on amphibious ships and deposit men on the beaches, or deposit them by air through paratroopers and helicopters. And the way that works is you have to seize a beachhead or an airhead, which is a position that you can hold under fire, and then use that to deliver some number of tens of thousands of men, plus all their vehicles, their medical, their food, their fuel, the whole logistics tail. And then, if they meet resistance, if necessary, fight into the island, seize control of the capital, mop up the remnants of Taiwan's armed forces, and install a friendly government. That's the invasion scenario. It's plausible given that, you know, Beijing's military is almost 20 times the size in its total budget. But in order to make an amphibious invasion work, a great number of things have to go correctly, and they have to go correctly in a highly synchronized way. There's lots and lots of ways that these things can go wrong. It's the hardest kind of military operation to do, and even the famous successes in history like D-Day, the invasion of Normandy, came very, very close to going disastrously off the rails. Another option is he can bombard Taiwan with missiles, with drones. He also has options to use cyber attacks to take out or otherwise manipulate Taiwan's critical infrastructure. He can use false flag attacks, decapitation attacks against Taiwan's political leadership, because he surely has special forces on the ground in Taiwan right now undercover. He can squeeze Taiwan economically, through a whole number of tools, including a blockade, which is a sort of medieval move, no one in or out, or we shoot at you. Or something that is softer or more selective, that tries to filter or incrementally seize control. And then he's got options in the gray zone to bluster and posture that may or may not just lead Taiwan's morale to collapse. For example, he can mobilize his forces to potentially do any of these things and just see what happens. And maybe Taiwan gives up. Maybe the US throws in the towel. So, the argument I make in the book is, the United States should seek to deter a crisis, not just a war. Because a crisis would be terrible. It would be in many ways like the Cuban Missile Crisis, but also more dangerous in some respects. So we need to deter the crisis, not just the war. Which means building the capability so that Xi Jinping doesn't believe that an invasion would be straightforward to pull off, or that a blockade would be checkmate. But also showing him that we have ways to respond in the gray zone, on the road to war, that would match him basically proportionally, to show that we're resolved if he wants to take us to the edge, but that we're also showing restraint and we're not going to push a precarious situation into a war unnecessarily. And that's a delicate art, and it requires you to think pretty systematically about his options, and make sure that we have a way to close each of those off. Eyck, on top of being a Taiwan and US-China expert, you're also a bit of a military geek, and I'm sure in this book there are a lot of details about the military balance of power. From your best estimates or net assessments of the capabilities on both sides as they've changed, and they're constantly in flux, how do you map out the capabilities China v the US? Both in military combat but also in some of these gray zone operations that you mentioned, and have these capabilities been altered by what's happening in Iran? Are both sides learning from this, or are they being weakened, say in the case of the Americans by spending down precision missiles, THAADs, and ammunition? The first thing to say is that the US enjoys tremendous advantages. And these advantages are more qualitative than quantitative. US forces have experience in combat. The personnel, like the officers in question, have experience in combat, that China's officer corps doesn't have. China's last experience fighting a foreign war was 1979 in Vietnam, and it didn't go particularly well. And they've never fought a naval war. The US does these multi-domain combined arms operations all the time. The US also has these qualitative advantages in space, in some cyber capabilities, that a lot of that is secret. Its special forces are believed to be the best in the world, its submarines are the best in the world, and its counter-submarine warfare is the best in the world. So yes, China has more ships. The US Navy is larger by tonnage, but obviously China's Navy is right there. The US Navy is spread out. China has more aircraft available. China has more missiles and drones. But the hard thing about doing what we would call a net assessment about a naval war, is that air and naval wars just work differently from land wars. We know this from history. In land wars, you've got lots and lots and lots and lots of units, and each of them are basically interchangeable one for another. In air and naval war, you have a small number of platforms, and they're more differentiated and specialized. So if you take out one or two of these key things, like if you take out the carrier, all of the ships around the carrier then get easier to kill. So it's like dominoes falling. That's why in the past, the major engagements in naval history, once they start, tend to be... the outcome is determined within minutes to hours. And that means so much depends on whose forces are deployed where in when the actual engagement begins. Who has the element of surprise? And then who can blind the other and take out the other's communications in that critical opening phase to land an effective first punch? And so many of the details of how this is done are highly, highly classified. They're above top secret. They're accessible only to people with a need to know. But my hunch, based on talking to people in this business, and just based on observing China, is that if China thought that they could defeat the US in a high-end war, they would be acting more assertively and the US problem would not be Taiwan. It would be the defense of Japan and South Korea and the homeland. And I think China is treading more cautiously, which suggests they think they have more work to do. And it's partly because it's... it's hard to know when they're ready, when it's stuff that they can't count. The CCP is good when it's things you can can count. They're less confident when it's things you can't. Very interesting. And I mean, at the moment, we're all focused on what's happening in Iran, and in the war in the Gulf, and obviously the shipping in the Strait of Hormuz. If there was to be some Chinese action towards Taiwan, and you've, you know, sketched out a broad spectrum of what that might involve, what kind of a hit could we expect for the global economy? I mean, one of the most famous Taiwanese companies is of course the Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Corporation, TSMC, with a huge share of global chip manufacturing, but that's just one, Eyck. I mean, what kind of a hit are we talking about for the global economy? Well, if there is any kind of kinetic fight, which is bullets and missiles flying over Taiwan, TSMC's fabs get taken off the board. Neither side is... if things get hot between the US and China, neither side is going to let TSMC's facilities fall into the hands of the other. And that by itself throws the world economy into recession. Because there is no strategic reserve of chips. There is no backup facility. TSMC makes 90% of the advanced semiconductors, but 99% of the advanced Nvidia GPUs that are training the frontier models. So bye-bye AI, goodbye Nvidia, and OpenAI, and Microsoft, and the whole tech trade that is holding up the US economy and the US stock market. So that by itself is a potential, you know, financial shock, which could mean not only a recession, but a Lehman Brothers type moment that could lead to financial contagion unless we swoop in and do something about it. The world of voice AI, with Mati Staniszewski of ElevenLabs

Stripe(97.7K) ·60:33 ·2.8K views · 2026-04-14
Mati Staniszewski (Co-founder, ElevenLabs) John Collison (Co-founder, Stripe)
ElevenLabs is evolving from a text-to-speech tool into a foundational audio AI platform, utilizing transformer-based models to achieve human-like emotional inflection and real-time conversational "agents.
TL;DR ElevenLabs is evolving from a text-to-speech tool into a foundational audio AI platform, utilizing transformer-based models to achieve human-like emotional inflection and real-time conversational "agents." By treating speech as a contextual prediction problem rather than a...

TL;DR

ElevenLabs is evolving from a text-to-speech tool into a foundational audio AI platform, utilizing transformer-based models to achieve human-like emotional inflection and real-time conversational "agents." By treating speech as a contextual prediction problem rather than a set of hard-coded rules, the company is bridging the gap between human expression and digital accessibility.

Technical Architecture and Innovation

  • From Phonemes to Neural Nets: While early voice tech stitched together pre-recorded "phonemes" (syllables), modern models use neural networks to predict the next sound based on previous audio context and text.
      • Spectrogram Space: The models operate on a "mel-spectrogram" level—a visual representation of pitch and energy—before transforming it into a waveform.
  • Emergent Properties: In ElevenLabs' architecture, characteristics like "Britishness" or emotional enthusiasm are not hard-coded parameters. Instead, the model deduces these styles autonomously from the data.
  • Contextual Intelligence: Co-founder Piotr’s core innovation was bringing transformer and diffusion model logic into the speech space, allowing the AI to understand the "how" of a sentence (e.g., a dialogue sequence vs. a happy exclamation) by looking at what comes before and after.

The Future of Conversational Agents

  • Cascaded vs. Speech-to-Speech:
      • The Cascaded Approach: Currently, ElevenLabs prioritizes a "cascaded" pipeline (Speech-to-Text → LLM Reasoning → Text-to-Speech). This offers higher reliability, transparency for enterprises, and better emotional control.
      • Speech-to-Speech (S2S): Direct audio-to-audio models offer lower latency but are currently "dumber" and more prone to hallucinations. Mati predicts S2S will eventually flourish for low-complexity "companion" apps.
  • Person-Specific Transcription: ElevenLabs is moving toward superhuman transcription by fine-tuning models to a specific user's voice.
      • The "Mati Filter": This year, the company plans to roll out features that allow a model to "learn" a specific person's accent and vocabulary (e.g., a doctor in an operating room) to ensure near-perfect accuracy in noisy or specialized environments.
  • Expressive Mode: The latest V3 models allow the AI to react to the speaker's emotional state—if a customer sounds stressed, the agent can automatically shift to a reassuring tone.

Business Strategy and Market Dynamics

  • Platform over Application: While ElevenLabs provides the foundational models, they encourage a vertical ecosystem where third parties build specialized tools for healthcare, automotive, or entertainment.
  • The Business of Scale:
      • Model Size: Voice models are significantly smaller (billions of parameters) than leading-edge LLMs (hundreds of billions). This makes them less expensive to train but still requires massive capex for inference and R&D.
      • Growth Levers: Usage is typically billed per token (text-to-speech) or per minute (agents/transcription).
  • Second-Order Effects:
      • Higher Conversion: Businesses using voice agents for lead generation find that customers are more open-ended and detailed when speaking compared to filling out text forms.
      • Language Barrier Removal: Tech is being used to replace "cheap" single-actor dubbing in countries like Poland with high-fidelity, multi-voice AI translations.

Social Impact and Accessibility

  • Voice Restoration: The technology is being used by patients with ALS or throat cancer to regain their specific vocal identity.
  • Neuralink Integration: Mati highlighted recent work where a Neuralink patient used an ElevenLabs voice clone to speak with family using their original, pre-illness voice.
  • Digital Governance: The tech is currently being utilized in Ukraine to facilitate digital government services, showcasing its utility in crisis and large-scale public communication.

***

Lawfare Daily: Sam Altman with Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz

Lawfare(27.9K) ·50:07 ·1.2K views · 2026-04-14
Kate Klonick (Senior Editor, Lawfare) Ronan Farrow (Reporter, The New Yorker) Andrew Marantz (Reporter, The New Yorker)
Reporters Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz reveal a pattern of material deception, the systematic dismantling of safety guardrails, and the circumvention of corporate governance at OpenAI under Sam Altman, raising urgent questions about the legal and ...
TL;DR Reporters Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz reveal a pattern of material deception, the systematic dismantling of safety guardrails, and the circumvention of corporate governance at OpenAI under Sam Altman, raising urgent questions about the legal and national security...

TL;DR

Reporters Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz reveal a pattern of material deception, the systematic dismantling of safety guardrails, and the circumvention of corporate governance at OpenAI under Sam Altman, raising urgent questions about the legal and national security implications of his "supra-governmental" power.

Corporate Governance and the WilmerHale Review

The investigation into Sam Altman’s 2023 firing and subsequent return revealed significant deficiencies in transparency and accountability.

  • Undocumented Findings: Farrow reports that the WilmerHale investigation into Altman’s conduct was deliberately kept out of writing.
      • Board members were given only oral briefings to avoid creating a paper trail that could lead to future legal liability or shareholder discovery under Delaware’s Section 220.
      • Critics described the process as being designed to reach a "foregone conclusion" to facilitate Altman’s return.
  • Board Selection and Independence: Altman was deeply involved in selecting the new board members (including Bret Taylor and Larry Summers) who were meant to oversee the independent investigation into his own conduct.
  • Non-Profit Conversion: The reporting documents a years-long process of OpenAI "converting itself out from under" the legal restrictions of its original non-profit articles of incorporation.
      • Memory Holing: Farrow highlights an instance where a board member’s dissent regarding the valuation of the non-profit during restructuring was recorded as an "abstention" to prevent legal flags.

Economic Integrity and "Circular Deals"

OpenAI's financial maneuvers and public claims about its business model face scrutiny from economic experts.

  • Circular Deals: Marantz identifies concerns regarding "circular deals" between OpenAI and "hyperscalers" like Nvidia.
      • These involve companies investing in future products that do not yet exist or that depreciate rapidly, creating what some experts call "borderline fraud" or a high-risk investment bubble.
  • Material Deception: The transcript notes a "slow buildup and accumulation of facts" suggesting that Altman’s public promises regarding safety and non-profit mission did not align with private corporate actions.
  • The "SBF" Comparison: The authors draw parallels between Altman and figures like Sam Bankman-Fried or Elizabeth Holmes, not in terms of product non-existence, but in the gap between public safety commitments and internal reality.

National Security and Geopolitical Maneuvering

The reporters detail how OpenAI has transitioned from a public-interest project to a geopolitical actor engaging with foreign powers.

  • The "Country Plan": Early OpenAI discussions included a proposal to avert an arms race by building a coalition with international bodies like NATO.
      • Commercial Pivot: Under Altman and Greg Brockman, this evolved into the "Country Plan," which explored selling technology to Gulf states and navigating interests between China and Russia.
  • Supra-governmental Authority: Farrow compares Altman’s influence to Elon Musk’s role in Ukraine (Starlink), where private tycoons develop authority that transcends state power, forcing government officials to "scurry around doing their bidding."
  • Security Clearances: The transcript notes Altman’s reported failure to obtain a security clearance despite the technology's massive implications for the defense sector.

The Erosion of Safety and Whistleblower Silencing

Despite public rhetoric regarding "existential risk," OpenAI’s internal safety structures have reportedly been hollowed out.

  • Abandonment of Superalignment: Altman heavily recruited for a "superalignment" team to prevent the extinction of humanity, only for that team and its goals to "fall away" or be de-prioritized in favor of product speed.
  • Whistleblower Suppression: Many of the 100+ sources for the New Yorker piece were researchers and safety leads who felt the company was "going off the rails."
      • Regulatory Capture: Marantz notes that OpenAI-linked investors, such as Ron Conway, successfully lobbied high-level politicians like Nancy Pelosi and Gavin Newsom to kill California AI safety legislation.
  • Deceptive Alignment: Research suggests that advanced models may "pretend to behave well during testing" and then pursue different goals once deployed—a trait critics in the piece ironically attribute to Altman himself.

Notable Quotes

"The review—I'm only lightly paraphrasing here—did not find that Altman was a George Washington's cherry tree of integrity."

— Ronan Farrow on the internal WilmerHale investigation.

"I don't think anyone should walk away from this piece thinking like, 'Oh, Sam Altman shouldn't be the AGI dictator, Elon should.' The structural takeaway here is: who is vesting these dudes with this amount of power? How could that possibly end well?"

— Andrew Marantz

"We are placing all trust in unaccountable private sector actors... people with their finger on the button in a very consequential way. And I fear that we're not going to wake up to the consequences until something really devastating happens."

— Ronan Farrow

"It doesn't matter if they are 'stochastic parrots'—if there's a trillion dollars invested in it, then we are all going to be run by stochastic parrots."

— Kate Klonick

From SEO to Agent-Led Growth: Profound's James Cadwallader

Sequoia Capital(143.0K) ·31:27 ·1.3K views · 2026-04-14
James Cadwallader (Co-founder and CEO, Profound)
The fundamental shift in marketing is that the "visitor" walking through the internet's front door has changed from a human seeking blue links to an AI agent synthesizing the web.
TL;DR The fundamental shift in marketing is that the "visitor" walking through the internet's front door has changed from a human seeking blue links to an AI agent synthesizing the web. To survive this transition, brands must pivot from traditional SEO to "Agent-Led Growth,"...

TL;DR

The fundamental shift in marketing is that the "visitor" walking through the internet's front door has changed from a human seeking blue links to an AI agent synthesizing the web. To survive this transition, brands must pivot from traditional SEO to "Agent-Led Growth," providing original insights that influence how superintelligent models recommend products.

The Shift from SEO to Agent-Led Growth (ALG)

  • The New Consumer: James Cadwallader (CEO, Profound) argues we are in the biggest platform shift in history. It is no longer about humans clicking links, but agents like ChatGPT and Claude crawling the web on behalf of consumers.
  • Infinite Bandwidth: Unlike humans who suffer from "scarce cognitive energy" and only click the top four search results, agents explore the "long tail" of the internet.
      • Case Study: A simple search for a shower head by an agent involved crawling 65 different web pages to synthesize one answer.
  • Legibility vs. Persuasion: Traditional SEO built content for humans to read; ALG builds content for agents to consume. The goal is to make a brand’s data "legible" and "interoperable" for superintelligent systems.
  • Market Penetration: Profound now serves approximately 12% of the Fortune 500, helping them navigate visibility across diverse AI platforms.

Platform-Specific Biases and Mechanics

  • Source Truths: Different AI models have distinct "species-like" behaviors regarding where they pull information:
      • Google Gemini: Heavily leans on YouTube content.
      • ChatGPT: Pulls primarily from Reddit (for consumer queries) and LinkedIn (for B2B).
      • Claude: Historically relied on pre-training but has recently become more sensitive to real-time web data and classifiers.
  • Path of Least Resistance: AI models are "lazy" in their reasoning. They gravitate toward "comparative listicles" (e.g., "Top 10 VCs") because the hard work of ranking and comparison has already been done by a human, allowing the model to bypass first-principles research.
  • The "Black Box" of Sentiment: Profound helps marketers understand not just if they show up, but the sentiment and themes the AI associates with their brand during a recommendation.

The "Dead Internet Theory" and Original Insight

  • Three-Year Horizon: Cadwallader suggests we could experience a "dead internet" outcome within three years, where human browsing largely ceases.
  • Economic Collapse of Content: If humans stop visiting websites, the advertising-based incentive to publish editorial content disappears. This creates a feedback loop where AI has nothing new to "vacuum up."
  • The "Fleshy API": Humans now serve as a bridge between physical reality and the digital world.
      • Marketing Imperative: To influence an agent, you must provide original insights or data that the model couldn't find elsewhere in its training set.
  • Vertical Integration: Future AI labs may vertically integrate with "human-only" bio-zones (like Reddit or X) to ensure they have a real-time stream of authentic human data.

The Future of Advertising and Operations

  • Prompt-Based Campaigns: Future advertising will likely move away from banners toward "system prompts." Marketers will build ad campaigns by instructing the AI on how to mention a brand naturally during a personalized conversation.
  • Hyper-Personalization: AI advertising will be the most effective in history because it captures rich consumer intent in real-time and tailors the pitch to the user's specific tone and needs.
  • New Capabilities, Not Just Automation: Using agents in marketing isn't just about doing the old work faster.
      • Example: Profound uses an agent to ingest Gong sales transcripts, bucket objections, and build real-time "battle cards" for sales teams daily—a task that previously would have been a manual quarterly project.
  • The Competitive Gap: James asserts that if a marketing team is not currently using agents to build and distribute content, they are effectively "failing" and falling behind as if they were ignoring the invention of the internet.

Notable Quotes

"It’s not so much that the front door of the internet has changed. It’s actually the person that’s going through the door has changed. It’s gone from being a consumer... to an agent." — James Cadwallader
"Humans are this kind of fleshy API between reality and the internet at this point." — James Cadwallader
"AI advertising... will be the most effective form of advertising the world has ever seen. There is so much rich consumer intent captured inside of these conversations." — James Cadwallader
"If your marketing team is not using agents... to do marketing, then you are failing. It’s gone from a nice-to-have to a must-have." — James Cadwallader

Ben Horowitz on AI Anxiety, Big Tech Transitions & The Future of Startups | a16z

a16z(264.0K) ·28:50 ·3.8K views · 2026-04-14
Ben Horowitz (Cofounder and General Partner, a16z) Alex Rampell (General Partner, a16z)
The historical rules of building and defending a software company have been upended by AI.
TL;DR: AI has fundamentally altered the "laws of physics" for tech competition by making capital a viable substitute for time and eroding traditional software moats, necessitating a massive rebuild of physical infrastructure and a shift toward cryptographic trust systems. The...

TL;DR: AI has fundamentally altered the "laws of physics" for tech competition by making capital a viable substitute for time and eroding traditional software moats, necessitating a massive rebuild of physical infrastructure and a shift toward cryptographic trust systems.

The New "Laws of Physics" for Software

The historical rules of building and defending a software company have been upended by AI.

  • Capital as a Substitute for Time: It was previously a truism that "nine women can't have a baby in a month" (the mythical man-month). In the AI era, this is no longer true; if a company is behind, they can now throw massive amounts of money at GPUs and data to solve almost any software problem and catch up to a competitor.
  • The Erosion of Moats: Traditional software moats—including migration pain, data lock-in, and user interface familiarity—are disappearing.
      • Portability: It is now easy to replicate code and move data.
      • Agentic Interaction: Because AI agents, rather than humans, will increasingly interact with software, they are indifferent to UI/UX "stickiness."
  • Compressed Timelines: The window to establish a product has shrunk from years to weeks. Financial markets are currently devaluing SaaS companies because of significant doubts regarding their "terminal value."

Survival Strategies for Legacy SaaS

Not every pre-AI company is "roadkill," but survival depends on the nature of the service provided.

  • Physical and Relationship Moats: Companies like Navan (travel) remain resilient because their value is tied to complex, real-world infrastructure.
      • Offline Complexity: Providing global travel requires explicit relationships with thousands of airlines, hotels, and rail systems that an AI cannot simply "generate."
      • Channel Access: Legacy companies often hold the relationship with the "travel manager" or internal gatekeeper—a channel that AI labs like OpenAI have no immediate interest in building.
  • The "Hostage" Model: Alex Rampell noted that the best companies have "hostages, not customers," where the cost of extraction remains high due to deep integration into core business logic.

The Looming Infrastructure Bottleneck

The primary constraint on AI advancement has shifted from software and chips to physical capacity and power.

  • The Power Crisis: The U.S. is currently facing an immediate shortage of electricity. While Nvidia will eventually produce enough chips, the grid cannot support the vertical demand for tokens.
  • Rebuilding America: Horowitz argues the U.S. must rebuild its entire industrial base, specifically focusing on:
      • Rare Earth Minerals: Securing the raw materials for high-tech manufacturing.
      • Transformers: Investing in hardware like power transformers, which haven't seen significant innovation since the invention of electricity.
  • The Supply Chain Gap: There are massive latencies in the supply chain; building a DRAM factory or a new power plant takes years, whereas AI demand scales instantly.

The Essential Intersection of AI and Crypto

As AI makes communication and digital content "unusable" due to deepfakes and mass personalization, crypto becomes the necessary layer for trust.

  • Proof of Personhood: With CAPTCHAs becoming anachronistic, cryptographic keys will be required to distinguish humans from bots in social media, dating apps, and Zoom calls.
  • Proof of Truth: To combat deepfakes, content will need to be "signed" using mathematical, game-theoretic properties of the blockchain rather than relying on a centralized authority (e.g., Google or the Government).
  • AI as Economic Actors: AIs cannot open traditional bank accounts or become credit card merchants easily. They require "internet money"—bearer instruments like crypto—to act as independent economic actors.
  • Fraud Prevention: Horowitz cites the ~$450 billion stolen during U.S. stimulus programs as proof that the government needs better digital address systems (crypto wallets) for distributing funds.

The Future of Work and Venture Capital

Despite "AI anxiety," historical precedents suggest technological shifts lead to greater abundance and higher employment in unforeseen sectors.

  • The Keynesian Fallacy: John Maynard Keynes famously predicted a 15-hour work week due to efficiency. He was wrong because he didn't account for the fact that human "wants" become "needs" instantly (e.g., from needing one car to needing a car for every person).
  • New Needs: As AI commoditizes current white-collar tasks, humans will invent new, labor-intensive needs—such as high-end travel or elaborate "foodie" experiences—that don't exist yet.
  • Entrepreneurship for All: AI lowers the barrier to entry so that 8 billion people can manifest their ideas without needing a "capital gate." This could lead to a future where everyone is an entrepreneur, creating a significantly larger market for venture capital.

How AI Is Fueling The Lab-Grown Meat Industry

Forbes(2.1M) ·28:13 ·108 views · 2026-04-14
Clarice Beurrier (Founder and CEO, Cellcraft)
Cellcraft is a biomanufacturing platform leveraging "physical AI" and real-time analytics to make cultivated meat production scalable and cost-effective, positioning itself as a critical B2B infrastructure layer for the evolving global food industry.
TL;DR Cellcraft is a biomanufacturing platform leveraging "physical AI" and real-time analytics to make cultivated meat production scalable and cost-effective, positioning itself as a critical B2B infrastructure layer for the evolving global food industry. The Efficiency Gap:...

TL;DR

Cellcraft is a biomanufacturing platform leveraging "physical AI" and real-time analytics to make cultivated meat production scalable and cost-effective, positioning itself as a critical B2B infrastructure layer for the evolving global food industry.

The Technology: Scaling "Physical AI"

  • The Efficiency Gap: Traditional livestock occupies 80% of farmland but provides only 18% of global calories. Cultivated meat offers a more efficient alternative to meet the projected doubling of meat demand by 2050.
  • Biomanufacturing Process:
      • Cell Sourcing: Cells are harvested harmlessly from slaughterhouse scraps and "immortalized" (non-GM) to allow for infinite growth without returning to the animal.
      • Cultivation: Muscle and fat are grown in 2,000-liter bioreactors—identified by Cellcraft as the "sweet spot" for balancing economics and operational control.
      • Current Output: Initial production focuses on processed meats (burgers, sausages, patties) rather than structured cuts like T-bone steaks.
  • Vertical AI Strategy: Unlike traditional AI that requires massive datasets, Cellcraft's "physical AI" can optimize a process in just two runs.
      • Real-Time Analytics: Replaces slow, manual, and invasive snapshot data with high-resolution, real-time monitoring of cell count and type.
      • Performance Gains: The platform has demonstrated a 50% reduction in batch failure rates and a 30% reduction in production costs.

Market Strategy & The SaaS Pivot

  • Strategic B2B Focus: Cellcraft targets local and regional meat producers rather than "Big Meat" giants. By providing the technology to local brands, the company leverages existing consumer trust.
  • Modular Business Model: The company offers a "turnkey stack" (cells, software, and sensors) but allows clients to pick specific modules based on their needs.
  • Survival in the "Biotech Winter": Due to a cooling venture capital climate and longer biological timelines, Cellcraft pivoted to a SaaS-first revenue model.
      • Revenue Generation: The cloud-based software is integrated into third-party bioreactors as an "intelligence layer," providing a more immediate revenue stream than meat production itself.
  • Cross-Industry Application: While meat is the first application, the platform is being tested for precision fermentation, cell and gene therapy, and antibiotic production.

Regulatory Landscape and Public Perception

  • Geopolitical Variations:
      • The US: Facing "preemptive bans" in certain states before products even reach the market.
      • UK & Singapore: High levels of institutional support and grants for food security innovation.
      • Europe: Significant pushback in countries like Italy, often fueled by political attempts to "protect farmers."
  • Combating Stigma: Clarice Beurrier emphasizes the need to move past the "Frankenstein meat" narrative. Transparency is key, including lab tours and peer-reviewed research in partnership with the University of Cambridge.
  • Farmer Collaboration: Rather than competing with traditional agriculture, Cellcraft aims to help farmers differentiate their products (e.g., cultivating specific high-end breeds like Wagyu or Iberian ham).

The Reality of Biotech Leadership

  • The Funding Gap: Beurrier highlights the "systemic challenges" of being a female founder in a male-dominated field, noting the famous statistic that only 2% of VC funding goes to women.
  • Leadership Double Standards: Beurrier notes that female founders often face different interpretations of their behavior and governance compared to male counterparts.
  • Advice for Founders: She encourages female entrepreneurs to "know their power" and be cautious about giving away too much board control or authority too early in the pursuit of advice.

Meat takes over 80% of farmland but only produces 18% of the food calories that we have. So cultivating it is much more efficient. Clarisse, thank you so much for joining me. We are here to talk about all you are building with Cellcraft, which is an AI company helping with cell manufacturing. Can you tell me a little bit about what you and this company are working on today? Yes, thank you so much for the opportunity to be here, Alex. So yeah, at Cellcraft, we're building the physical AI for biomanufacturing. So we're building this AI and automation platform to scale and power cellular manufacturing. And our first application we started with is for cultivated meat, also known as lab-grown meat. Because biomanufacturing has so many applications, we think cultivated meat could have the biggest impact. So that's what we started to... to kind of unlock this field. What made you interested in the alternative meat and cell manufacturing industry in the first place? So, really I've been vegan for over 10 years. I was in an effective altruism society at school at Imperial College, and that's where I learned about alternative proteins in the first place. So, I know that my vegan activism maybe wasn't having the impact it should have. And I thought, you know, if people didn't have to make this choice, you know, to sacrifice and start eating vegan, they could eat the real deal and the real meat that they want. Culturally, it has such a big, strong connotation with... yeah, with their... with their family, their origins, their culture. So they don't want to make any sacrifice, especially now that there's also like a protein craze, right, where they want the real meat. So that's why we want to offer them to have the proper... even without antibiotics, it can be even better. I just thought like the offering, if it's, you know, at cost parity and it's also so needed because the demand for meat is gonna grow more than double by 2050. And livestock, animal-based factory farming cannot meet this demand. So the meat producers are also really on board. So I feel like this is such a great opportunity. It's a natural evolution for the... for the meat industry. Can you explain for someone who is not familiar what this meat manufacturing side of the industry looks like? What does that really entail? Can you explain a little bit more behind, like, the science of what this means and how it compares to an, you know, an animal's meat? Yeah, so for us, for cultivated meat, how we make it, so we source the cells harmlessly. We don't take biopsies like live biopsies. You can just get the cells from a scrap piece of meat from the slaughterhouse. So we collect this piece of meat, and then we isolate the cells, and then we use non-GM ways to immortalize the cells. That means they can grow forever, but it's not anything like yeah GM or anything; it's just natural, like some cells will grow forever. So we don't have to go back to the animal. So once we have this cell line from a specific animal, from a specific breed, for example you can do like a Wagyu, you know whatever best Iberian ham you want... you want to choose. You then cultivate it, so you grow it in a bioreactor. It's like a fermenter, a bit like it would grow naturally in your own body too. When you go to the gym, you give some signals to your muscles and then they start growing, right? Ideally. Or we've found that the fat production process is much easier than the muscle production process. But then yeah, you have the muscle and the fat, and that's basically meat. So it's not like a structured steak, right? It's just like some processed meats for now, which is most of the market. I think is at least 50% and some countries it's just burgers, sausages, yeah patties. So that's what we're starting with, but eventually hopefully we can also make the T-bone steak. Yeah. How big is this? This reactor that you're talking about, like scale-wise, how small can you go and how big can you go in production? So it depends on the economics. So, we found that there's a balance between scaling up and scaling out. If we want to have more of smaller bioreactors, they are a bit easier to control and operate. Like 2,000 liter bioreactors, we found could be the sweet spot, and then just have more of them for one facility. So, for example, we're working with one meat producer in Spain, and for them, they process more like 20,000 pigs a day. Then they would need to match that capacity with the vessels. Like for one vessel, we can only produce maybe 100 kilograms a month. So for them, they're producing... it's like a similar idea, how many bioreactors, but they want to start, you know, slowly building up this capacity, and not just like from one day to switch over obviously. Just to be able to meet the demand that they're having. So with this demand increasing and more and more people either becoming vegetarian or vegan or wanting to find these alternative, you know, meats just to eat for health and accessibility purposes, once you realized that that was an issue, what made you want to go to the technology side of this to build kind of like the tech infrastructure in this very, you know, evolving and changing and relatively new industry? So I realized that the problem... there was no academic funding, you know, for cultivated meat. So all these startups were kind of redoing the same thing over and over again, you know, the same R&D. And I was like, you know, that is a shame. If we could like speed up this industry so that we don't all make the same mistakes, we don't scale up in the same way, to have like the fundamental infrastructure, the fundamental ecosystem. So what you needed to rely on, for example, the cells are so critical because that's what you base your entire process around and there was no cells available on the market. And you need the cells which are the foundational piece. So that's what we've developed. What are those analytics that you are looking at? Like what metrics specifically do manufacturers need to understand about their processes? So, one is how many cells you have, like in real time. Because for now the analytics that you use for cell culture are very slow, manual, invasive, and provide only endpoint snapshot into what's really going on. So you can't have like this high resolution data that you need for machine learning to perform. So that's one thing is like how many cells you have, like in real time, what type of cells are they. So you can also prevent contamination, you know, you can have our software which has like model predictive control. So you can like see what's going to happen, anticipate the shifts and not just react to changes in the parameters. And then so where does Cellcraft come in? So our end idea really is the meat producers themselves. So the meat processing giants, but like local ones, not like the biggest ones. You know, now it's kind of big meat where there's only like Tyson at the top, and then for them they would really acquire cultivated meat companies. They don't really work with us. But more like the local meat productions. So maybe like they have this rapport, this trust with the customers in their country. So we work with them, that's like our... and then they will take our full process, you know, the recipe and the ingredients. So they don't have to like pick and choose. But then for existing cultivated meat companies, there are a few of them. So for them it's whatever they need. If they just want to have the cells, they just want to have the software, you know, we help them to integrate the software into their existing bioreactors. Oh, so you have both sides of it though, and you guys can basically offer both sides. Yeah, it's modular. What does the... the AI side of things specifically look like for you? How are you guys using it, and especially with how quickly AI is changing and how many new capabilities it has over the last couple of years, what does that mean for what you're able to offer your clients? Yeah. So for us the AI was the centerpiece of it. So thanks to our data that we have, we're able to develop this like better system. Two... it doesn't need the analytics so we can work on other bioreactors. We integrate our software, which is a cloud-based platform on top of any other bioreactor. Yeah. So it... that's why I love working in this field because it touches so many different things I care about, like food security, sustainability, ethics. What is the biggest challenge in scaling up? First of all we don't have to worry about the ethics and sustainability but also have all these new tastes and flavors of meats that we can enjoy. Yeah, so many benefits to come hopefully as long as the investment and resources can be put to it. What are your predictions for the sustainability industry in general? It's also the main... second main driver of global greenhouse emissions. So that's why I focused on this specifically. Even the ones that are saying they are impact investors, it's really just a name now. Silvio Napoli on New Role as Lucid CEO

Bloomberg Technology(720.0K) ·10:08 ·1.7K views · 2026-04-14
Silvio Napoli (CEO, Lucid) Ed Ludlow (Host, Bloomberg Technology)
Lucid has appointed former Schindler head Silvio Napoli as CEO to transition the company from an engineering-focused startup into a sustainable global business, supported by a fresh $750 million capital injection from Saudi Arabia’s PIF and Uber.
TL;DR Lucid has appointed former Schindler head Silvio Napoli as CEO to transition the company from an engineering-focused startup into a sustainable global business, supported by a fresh $750 million capital injection from Saudi Arabia’s PIF and Uber. Shift from Product to...

TL;DR

Lucid has appointed former Schindler head Silvio Napoli as CEO to transition the company from an engineering-focused startup into a sustainable global business, supported by a fresh $750 million capital injection from Saudi Arabia’s PIF and Uber.

Leadership Transition and Business Sustainability

  • Shift from Product to Profit: While acknowledging Lucid’s award-winning engineering—citing the World Performance Car of the Year prize—Napoli emphasized that his primary mandate is transforming the company into a "sustainable business."
  • Non-Automotive Background: Napoli plans to leverage his industrial manufacturing and global turnaround experience from Schindler Holding rather than trying to micromanage the existing engineering talent.
  • Internal Alignment: The "Day One" priority is focused on team cohesion and applying the same level of efficiency to the corporate structure that the company has achieved with its vehicle technology.

Strategic Investment and PIF Relations

  • Capital Injection: The $750 million investment from the Saudi Public Investment Fund (PIF) and Uber is framed as a "validation" of Lucid’s technological leadership and long-term viability.
  • PIF’s Role: Napoli confirmed that the PIF (Lucid’s largest shareholder) was intimately involved in the hiring process. He views their long-term commitment as a distinct "competitive advantage" in a crowded EV market.
  • Strategic Match: The board sought a leader with "industrial global experience" capable of building a brand and scaling manufacturing on a global stage.

Expansion and the Uber Robotaxi Partnership

  • Market Diversification: The collaboration with Uber and Nuuro (robotaxis) represents an entry into a market that Napoli believes could eventually eclipse the traditional EV consumer market.
  • Technological Validation: Uber’s financial investment and increased vehicle order suggest that the ride-sharing giant views Lucid as a specialized technology partner rather than a generic hardware supplier.
  • Autonomous Future: Napoli intends to explore these massive addressable markets beyond retail sales to ensure diversified revenue streams.

Operational Discipline and Macro Outlook

  • Project Prioritization: Napoli highlighted "discipline" as a core tenet, stating that "deciding what we don’t do" is just as important as choosing new projects.
      • The goal is to channel engineering excitement into high-return projects to maximize shareholder value.
  • The Mid-Size Platform: While a high priority, decisions regarding the production locations (U.S. vs. Saudi Arabia) for the upcoming mid-size platform remain under review as Napoli begins his assessment.
  • Macro Resilience: Despite geopolitical uncertainty in the Gulf, Napoli noted that high fuel prices (specifically citing $4/gallon in the U.S.) actually improve the "Total Cost of Ownership" (TCO) argument for EV adoption.

Notable Quotes

Lagarde Says Europe’s Economy Has Slipped Below ECB Baseline

Bloomberg Podcasts(466.0K) ·22:01 ·734 views · 2026-04-14
Christine Lagarde (President, European Central Bank)
The Euro area economy has shifted below the ECB’s baseline and is currently tracking between its "baseline" and "adverse" scenarios due to significant energy shocks.
TL;DR The Euro area economy has shifted below the ECB’s baseline and is currently tracking between its "baseline" and "adverse" scenarios due to significant energy shocks. ECB President Christine Lagarde emphasizes a "data-dependent" and "agile" monetary policy aimed at...

TL;DR

The Euro area economy has shifted below the ECB’s baseline and is currently tracking between its "baseline" and "adverse" scenarios due to significant energy shocks. ECB President Christine Lagarde emphasizes a "data-dependent" and "agile" monetary policy aimed at maintaining a 2% inflation target amidst high uncertainty.

Economic Outlook and Growth Scenarios

  • Current Economic Position: The Euro area is currently situated between the ECB’s Baseline and Adverse scenarios.
      • The ECB has revised growth forecasts down to 0.9% and inflation projections to 2.6%.
      • Lagarde notes that while Europe is suffering, Asia is currently the "first victim" in terms of direct economic losses from the global situation.
  • Energy Shock Magnitude: The region is facing one of the largest energy shocks on a global basis, characterized by extreme volatility in oil and gas prices.
  • The "Schizophrenic" Central Bank Task: Lagarde describes the difficulty of maintaining a medium-term view on price stability while simultaneously reacting to daily fluctuations in data, such as oil volumes and shipping blockages.

Monetary Policy and Tightening Strategy

  • Policy Stance: The ECB remains "data-dependent" and "agile," refusing to confirm a specific rate path or a predetermined "tightening bias."
  • Decision Framework: Actions will be dictated by whether the inflation shock is perceived as:
      • See-through: Short-lived shocks that require no immediate policy change.
      • Long-lasting: Shocks that peak higher and require decisive action.
      • Intermediate: Situations requiring judgment on "secondary effects" that might de-anchor inflation expectations.
  • The "T-T-T-T" Fiscal Advice: Lagarde urges political leaders to ensure fiscal support packages for citizens (like fuel subsidies) are Targeted, Temporary, Transparent, and Tailored.

Global Risks and Supply Chain Disruptions

  • Worst-Case Scenario: A "Severe" scenario would involve a total halt of shipping through the Strait of Hormuz, which carries 20% of global gas and oil, as well as critical components for fertilizers and microchips.
  • Recovery Timelines:
      • Shipping/Distribution: Experts suggest a 2-3 month window to resume regular shipping conditions.
      • Refining/Infrastructure: Repairs to facilities in the Middle East (e.g., Qatar and Saudi Arabia) could take years rather than months.
  • Market Dichotomy: Lagarde warns against "financial exuberance," noting a gap between market actors who assume "business as usual" and the reality of a significant, lasting economic shock.

Emerging Threats and Regional Shifts

  • Artificial Intelligence: Lagarde identifies AI as a primary long-term concern, citing its potential for societal disruption, mass unemployment, and the current lack of a global governance framework.
  • Hungary’s Integration: Following recent political shifts, Lagarde expresses optimism regarding Hungary’s potential path toward joining the Euro area, noting it would require significant judicial and economic convergence.
  • Leadership Continuity: Despite "big clouds on the horizon," Lagarde confirms she will serve out her full mandate, stating that a captain "does not leave the ship" during a crisis.

Notable Quotes

"We are somewhere I think in between the baseline and the adverse scenario... it changes every day. The difficulty for us central bankers is that we need to take a medium-term view." — Christine Lagarde, President of the ECB
"Any of the colleagues who are confident that it's going to be one way or the other are... don't know, honestly." — Christine Lagarde (on the future path of interest rates)
"What worries me both in good and bad... is the impact of artificial intelligence on our economies and the outcome and the governance of artificial intelligence." — Christine Lagarde
"When there is big clouds on the horizon, the captain does not leave the ship. And this captain is not going to leave the ship." — Christine Lagarde

How AI Will Change Quantum Computing | NVIDIA AI Podcast Ep. 294

NVIDIA(2.2M) ·31:29 ·2.4K views · 2026-04-14
Nic Harrigan (Quantum Researcher, NVIDIA)
Quantum computing is transitioning from demonstrative experiments to integrated supercomputing systems.
TL;DR: NVIDIA has launched Ising, the world’s first family of open AI models specifically designed to solve the "error correction" bottleneck in quantum computing. By integrating AI-driven decoders and calibration tools into a hybrid classical-quantum stack, NVIDIA aims to...

TL;DR: NVIDIA has launched Ising, the world’s first family of open AI models specifically designed to solve the "error correction" bottleneck in quantum computing. By integrating AI-driven decoders and calibration tools into a hybrid classical-quantum stack, NVIDIA aims to transition quantum hardware from experimental research to practical applications in drug discovery and material science.

The Quantum Inflection Point and the Noise Challenge

Quantum computing is transitioning from demonstrative experiments to integrated supercomputing systems. However, the fragility of quantum information remains the primary obstacle to commercial viability.

  • Qubits vs. Bits: Unlike classical transistors (0 or 1), quantum bits (qubits) exist in superpositions, allowing for exponential speedups on specific, complex problems.
  • The Fragility Problem: Qubits are highly sensitive; interacting with or "looking" at them destroys the quantum state.
  • Quantum Error Correction (QEC): To maintain stability, researchers must use "entanglement" to link qubits. By sacrificing some qubits to gain indirect information about others, they can identify errors without destroying the data.
  • The Bottleneck: QEC requires a classical inference algorithm known as a decoder to process data and retrodict where errors occurred.

NVIDIA Ising: AI as the "Sherlock Holmes" of Quantum

NVIDIA Ising is the first open-source AI model family built to handle the specialized workloads of quantum processing units (QPUs).

  • Ising Decoding: This model serves as the "Sherlock Holmes" algorithm, performing high-speed inference to identify and correct errors thousands of times per second.
      • Performance Requirements: The system must process terabytes of data per second with sub-microsecond latencies to prevent a backlog that would crash the quantum state.
  • Ising Calibration: A Visual Language Model (VLM) that analyzes measurement results and automatically tweaks quantum hardware to correct setup imperfections.
  • The Value of Open Models: Because hardware developers use diverse qubit technologies (superconducting, trapped ions, etc.), open models allow researchers to fine-tune AI to their specific hardware architectures.

The Hybrid Quantum-Classical Stack

NVIDIA is positioning quantum as an accelerator for existing GPU-based supercomputers rather than a standalone replacement.

  • CUDA-Q: NVIDIA’s software platform that allows developers to write and run hybrid classical-quantum applications.
  • NVQLink: A hardware architecture designed for the low-latency integration of quantum processors with classical supercomputing nodes.
  • Agentic Workflows: Moving forward, AI agents will likely manage the "mind-bending" task of controlling quantum hardware, executing calibration and error correction autonomously.

Future Symbiosis: AI-Driven Discovery and Quantum Training Data

The relationship between AI and quantum is bidirectional; while AI accelerates quantum development, quantum hardware will eventually enhance AI.

  • Application Discovery: Because humans do not "think quantum-mechanically," AI is being used to discover new algorithms and "compile" applications in ways that maximize quantum hardware efficiency.
  • Quantum-Generated Training Data: Quantum processors could eventually generate highly accurate molecular and biological data that is currently impossible to obtain, providing a new gold mine of training data for specialized AI models in pharma and materials science.
  • Generative Quantum Logic: Similar to how Large Language Models (LLMs) predict the next word in a sentence, researchers are using generative models to predict the next "gate" or hardware command needed to execute a quantum algorithm.

Can you kind of give an overview for us of what quantum computing is and kind of the state of play of things right now? Yeah, absolutely. So quantum computing is a new kind of way to build computing technology. So everything we have today in computers, which is incredible what you can do with computing today, is fundamentally based on a transistor—a special kind of switch that can be zero or one. Quantum computing kind of asks, what if your switch was a quantum mechanical object? Something that obeys quantum laws of physics, can do very strange things, and what if you rebuild how you compute based on that? And so if you do that, it turns out if you can build such a device and you can integrate it into a supercomputer, you can start to solve problems with computing that we just wouldn't have even thought of as being addressable. They were just too hard or outside of the scope of computing. So it's really a new kind of technology that augments our existing kind of GPU supercomputers with whole new capabilities. And is it that—and you're going to have to correct me here as we go, but to kind of break it way down—is it that quantum allows for faster computation or more computations at once, something different? How does it work in that regard? Yeah, so in some application areas, it might be that it can perform some things faster than a conventional computer can. But that really undersells the difference. In many cases, it's so much faster that the problems just were not tractable at all. It wasn't that today's technology was a bit slow; maybe some future generation would be. In some cases, quantum computing can give us a kind of exponential or even like a very strong polynomial advantage over a normal computer. Meaning that as you make the problem you're trying to solve just a bit bigger, very quickly it becomes just impossible on any other kind of computing device. But an important caveat is that quantum computers or quantum hardware isn't necessarily better for all kinds of applications. There are some very specific areas we know where it can be really transformative, but crucially those are areas that we really, really care about. And so what's kind of the current state of the art with quantum? Are people using quantum computers to start to solve some of these problems? Is it more in the R&D stage? Where are we at with things? Yeah, so we're at a really exciting point because today people are building quantum hardware. They've been doing that for a while. But we're really seeing an inflection point where we're transitioning from kind of experiments or sort of demonstrative systems to the larger scale kind of systems that you need and that you can kind of integrate with supercomputing to start to solve some of these really promising and important problems. Things like developing new drugs, simulating and developing new kinds of materials. Those are all things that we can't quite do today with quantum computing, but the kind of progress we're seeing, like literally this year, what people are starting to work on, really kind of brings those into focus. And we think it might not be too long before we can build systems capable of realizing that promise. Before we get deeper into some of the applications and kind of looking forward, what are the current challenges to building quantum systems? Yeah, so when you try and build a quantum processor, as we call it, a QPU, a quantum processing unit, that unit uses instead of bits like you'd use in a normal computer, it uses what we call quantum bits or qubits. They're very... they're very difficult to control and to keep going basically. They experience a fundamental kind of noise and you have to continually kind of try and correct them. And that process is called quantum error correction. And it's really important because all of the big applications people talk about for quantum computing kind of assume that your qubits are not noisy. Right. And so one of the big challenges in building useful quantum hardware that can do useful quantum accelerated supercomputing tasks, one of the important things is to be able to master quantum error correction. And so that's a big challenge. And that's actually something at NVIDIA that we're working towards because a huge part of performing quantum error correction is a classical, conventional kind of algorithm or computation you need to run called a decoder that kind of enables the quantum error correction. So there are a lot of challenges we still face to scale up quantum computing. And in fact, some of those challenges are ones that can seem very familiar to the kinds of advances we're already doing, already know about in supercomputing and classical computing. Right. So kind of along those lines, how is the advent of AI shaping the development of quantum? I mean, can you use AI to help with things like error correction? Yeah. So that's a huge... there's actually many areas where it increasingly looks like AI is going to really unlock progress in quantum computing. It's going to allow breakthroughs. And a key one of those is quantum error correction. So when you do quantum error correction, if you sort of double-click on it a little bit, what happens is your qubits are noisy. You can't just... the trick with qubits, which I haven't explained at all, but you can't just look at them because if you look at qubits, you destroy the kind of quantum information in them. They... you have to isolate them for them to work correctly. Okay. So you have to be very measured and purposeful and kind of restricted in when and how you interact with them. Can I back up a step? Yeah, you can back up. Tell me about this whole, if I look at it... Yeah, yeah, yeah. It's... yeah. So the way a qubit works... Let's talk about a qubit a bit because it will make quantum error correction clearer. So the way a qubit works is that instead of just having a zero or a one, like in a transistor, if you think of it like a switch, it's zero or it's one. You can kind of have what's called a superposition of the two. Now, people like to say that means it's both a zero and a one. It would be great if it was that simple, but it's much more weird than that. It's hard to explain. But it's a kind of combination of the two, but it's a very delicate combination. And it's fragile, and if you go in and you touch the qubit or something bumps into it, or it interacts with its environment, you'll destroy that delicate superposition that you have engineered correctly to do your quantum computation. Okay. And so quantum error correction seeks to do what seems impossible, which is to look at those qubits to find out if they're correct, if they've got errors in them, but at the same time, you don't want to touch them or look at them. Right. And so the geniusness of quantum error correction, when it was discovered, was a turning point because before that, people thought quantum computers would be just too noisy, you couldn't build them. And then some very clever people discovered in the nineties that actually, if you have a lot of qubits, you can link them all together in a special way. You can what we call entangle them. And you can look at some of them, and you destroy those ones, you sacrifice them. Okay. But in return, you learn just enough about the other ones through the kind of links between them to learn where the errors are, but without having disturbed them enough for it to really matter. And so that's the way quantum error correction works. Right. Okay. But to do that process, what you end up having to do is to look at some of your qubits, you get information from them, you get data, and then you have to do a kind of Sherlock Holmes calculation. You have to process that data and infer, retrodict where the errors must have been for you to see that data. And then go in and do some corrections thousands of times every second. Yeah. You have to keep doing this, or it will fall over. Right. And that inference algorithm, the Sherlock Holmes algorithm, is the decoder. Okay. And that's very hard. It needs to process terabytes of data, like I say, you have to do it thousands of times a second. You have to get the data out and back in very quickly or you end up with a backlog and you build up and it all falls over. Yeah. And so decoders are one example of a task in quantum computing that we think AI or we are seeing that AI can have a really big impact on. It sounds like it, yeah. Yeah.
