TL;DR

Markets are nearing record highs as traders focus on resilient bank earnings and a potential de-escalation in the Middle East, with Iran considering a pause in maritime shipments to keep peace talks on the table.

Banking & Financial Markets

  • Mixed Earnings Results: Major financial institutions reported a complex quarter characterized by strong trading but cautious outlooks.
      • JPMorgan Chase: Reported record trading revenue (over $11B) but shares were tempered by a slight reduction in net interest income (NII) guidance to $103B.
      • Citigroup: CEO Jane Fraser’s turnaround showed momentum with record equity trading figures (over $2B) and a "clean beat" on results.
      • Wells Fargo: Missed on interest income and provided first-ever granular disclosure of private credit exposure ($36B), reassuring investors that 0% losses have been seen in that segment so far.
      • BlackRock: Captured $130B in client cash during Q1, driven by a surge in ETF inflows.
  • Market Resilience: The S&P 500 is within 1% of all-time highs, pricing out war risk despite Brent crude remaining above $90/barrel.
      • "Business as Usual": Analysts noted a significant shift where downside protection (puts) is being abandoned in favor of chasing the rally in mega-cap tech and cyclicals.
      • Labor Market Strength: Analysts and CEOs suggest the consumer remains resilient as long as the labor market doesn't "crack," despite higher prices at the pump.

Geopolitical Shifts & Energy

  • Strait of Hormuz Pause: Iran is reportedly considering a short-term halt to shipments through the Strait of Hormuz.
      • De-escalation Goal: The move is intended to avoid US maritime retaliation and protect fragile peace talks ahead of a looming ceasefire deadline.
      • Diplomatic Channels: Potential face-to-face meetings between the US and Iran are being rumored for Geneva or Pakistan, with the US reportedly shifting demands to a 20-year moratorium on uranium enrichment rather than a permanent ban.
  • Energy Impact: While oil prices have dropped from recent peaks of $117, the ECB and other agencies remain cautious about long-term damage to production facilities in Qatar and Saudi Arabia, which could take years to repair.

Global Policy & Macro Strategy

  • Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent on "Growth First": Speaking at the IIF, Bessent advocated for a radical refocusing of the World Bank and IMF.
      • Abandoning Climate Targets: Bessent argued that "luxury beliefs" regarding climate change should not hinder the core mission of lifting nations out of poverty through energy abundance (including nuclear and fossil fuels).
      • Global Imbalances: He warned that the world cannot sustain China’s $1 trillion trade surplus and praised Argentina’s "success" under Javier Milei.
  • ECB Outlook (Christine Lagarde): In an exclusive interview, Lagarde placed the current economy between "baseline" and "adverse" scenarios.
      • Monetary Agility: She emphasized that the ECB has no "tightening bias" but remains data-dependent, focusing on growth risks to specific sectors like fishing and transport.
      • AI Governance: Lagarde expressed deep concern over the lack of a global governance framework for Artificial Intelligence, citing its potential for massive social disruption.

Corporate M&A and Industry Innovation

  • Aviation "Shock": United Airlines CEO Scott Kirby reportedly pitched a merger with American Airlines to senior officials.
      • Scale: The tie-up would create the world’s largest airline.
      • Regulatory Outlook: While traditionally a "non-starter," the pitch to the Trump administration suggests a belief in a more lenient anti-trust environment.
  • Healthcare & Pharma:
      • Johnson & Johnson: Raised its full-year guidance following the successful launch of *Tremfya* (a psoriasis pill) and strong performance in its med-tech and cardiovascular units.
      • HSA Expansion: Startup *TrueMed* reports a massive surge in Gen Z and Millennial interest in using tax-advantaged Health Savings Accounts for preventative wellness (supplements, gym memberships, etc.).
  • Ultra-Luxury Trends: Rolls-Royce unveiled "Project Nightingale," a $3M–$5M invite-only EV limited to 100 clients. CEO Chris Brownridge noted that ultra-high-net-worth demand is shifting from "objects" to "experiences and customization."

Development policy, like avoiding death, costs just billions or maybe even just millions of dollars and saves lives right now. As global finance chiefs weigh the fallout from the Iran war, Bloomberg's Francine Lacqua sits down exclusively with the President of the ECB, Christine Lagarde. Let's listen in. Objectives of those projects will be. How long it will last and how fast the situation will be recovered. And we keep looking at current data as they come in. And thanks to Bloomberg, for instance, this comes in on a very regular basis. We try to figure out where we are relative to the baseline and where we are relative to the various scenarios that we have laid out. Yeah, and I think you laid it out as three scenarios: Baseline, Adverse, and Severe. I would call the baseline "the baseline" and the other two "the scenarios." So where are we now? We are in between the baseline and the adverse. So we have baseline, adverse, and severe. Currently, when I say "currently," you have to look at the price of the barrel, the price of the various categories of fuel, the price of futures, and all of that applied to gas as well to determine where we are exactly relative to these two. But we are somewhere, I think, in between the baseline and the adverse scenario. So how quickly can that change from baseline to scenarios? It changes every day. The difficulty for us central bankers is that we need to take a medium-term view. This is how we define our target. This price stability that defines our mandate is done by reference to the medium-term results. And at the same time, facts, data, the number of ships blocked here, and the volume of oil extracted varies almost on a daily basis. So we need to do this somehow schizophrenic exercise of keeping our eyes on the medium term and making sure that we deliver on our mandate, but at the same time checking the data almost daily. So what does it mean for monetary policy? Does the baseline already warrant a hike? What it means for monetary policy is that we have to be two things. We have to be completely agile and ready to move in the direction that is required. And second, we have to be data dependent, as we have repeatedly said. But it does not predicate, as we speak today, Francine, that we will go in one direction or the other. It certainly doesn't determine a rate path that I can confirm today. And any of the colleagues who are confident that it's going to be one way or the other don't know, honestly. But don't forget one thing: the baseline and all our scenarios are designed and formulated with the incorporation of what the market assumes. No other monetary policy or fiscal policy decision affects the baseline or either of the two scenarios. Do you think there's a tightening bias at the ECB? No, I think we have a compass which indicates price stability predicated on financial stability. Those are the two; I wouldn't call them a bias. How different is it from the shock in 2022, and how different will your response be? Well, it's different from 2022 in many ways. If you look back, inflation was completely different, interest rates were completely different, and the shock was a combination of supply and demand. It was a vastly different situation. I think Europe was affected in a much more significant way then than it is at the moment because we had this sudden stop that came out of the unjustifiable war of Russia against Ukraine, which brought to a halt the delivery of gas. That was very sudden. I mean, at the time, central banks were blamed for acting too slow. Is there a danger—I mean, how does that impact again your thinking this time? Look, I think we've said very clearly that we would need data to act, but that we would not hesitate to act. I think that really captures well the position that we have. We need the data in order to analyze whether it's a "see through"—that it's going to be short-lived, and we will get back to the past, if you will, although I don't think that's actually possible. We need all that. So it's either the "see through," which doesn't require that we take any particular decision, or it's going to be long-lasting. It will peak higher, and it will require action on our part. Or it will be somewhere in between, which will then require judgment on the part of the Governing Council to decide whether or not the indirect effect and the risk of secondary effect might de-anchor expectations, which then would require action as well. So I was at a dinner yesterday with the Treasury Secretary, who was saying that actually, he thinks this will be short-lived. But he's a political leader as well as being a terribly competent person. He is a political leader. I am not a political leader anymore. Our job as central bankers is to stay in our lane and to observe our framework. We have one, thank you very much. We must identify whether or not we are in a situation of short-lived "look through," or long-lasting "act," and possibly decisively, or in between. The "in between" requires a very subtle identification of how much and when in order to make sure that inflation expectations are anchored and we do not get the secondary effects. That's the job. It's the difficulty of what we have to do at the moment, while at the same time very seriously looking at the medium term and being attentive to the short term. And so, your gut feeling for what we're living through now—you'll act quicker than in 2022? I don't have a gut feeling, you know. When I hear the music of "this is short-lived, we will go through that, you can see through," first of all, we decide what we have to do. Point number two, to explain to the fisherman who cannot go out to sea because he cannot pay for his petrol, or when I think of the household that doesn't know whether they'll be able to pay for the refilling of the fuel tank, or whether I think of the guys who have to drive to work and see the price of oil at the pump going up and up—I take the "see through" story into account. But this is not something that you can actually explain. It requires, from our perspective, in order to align in the most effective way monetary and fiscal policies, a dialogue with the fiscal policy leaders. We ask them to be targeted, temporary, transparent, and tailored—all those "TTTTs." When they design support packages or help for the people who are the fisherman, the housekeeper, the household, or the guy who is driving his car, it must be tailored. Given where we are now, what does the worst-case scenario for growth and inflation look like? The worst-case scenario is war, in any event. As former head of the IMF, I have seen successful programs being completely wiped out by war. So war is, in any circumstances, the worst outcome. If you talk in strict economic terms and the movement of goods, it's the Strait of Hormuz not being open for shipping and transportation. You have 20% of gas and oil going through that, as well as the ingredients necessary for fertilizers, microchips, and what have you, not going through the strait. So what's the pain threshold for the Euro area economy that the ECB would have to act on? Is it inflation at 3% or 4%? Look, we have forecasts for inflation that vary between 2.6%, which is the baseline that we have, and a little north of 4%. Based on what I told you earlier, which is "see through," strong decisive reaction, or somewhere in between—depending on the factors that we see, we will adjust. Because we will be agile. But one thing for sure is that we will keep our 2%. Madame Lagarde, how worried are you about the discrepancy between spot and future oil prices and potentially tighter markets that we're pricing in? I think it's the whole dilemma that we have about short term and medium term. The more anxious and worried economic actors are about the resolution of this crisis, the more pressure there will be on the spot market. How much do you blame the US administration for what's happening to the European economy? It's not my job to point fingers or to associate this or that with blame. I'm more in the hope section myself. I very much hope that people can sit at the table, can come to their senses, and understand that it will be in the best interest of the global economy and the people that a war settlement is reached—and that's not just a ceasefire. We need to navigate through the straits of the world. That has ramifications with international law, but let's not go there. I'm a reformed lawyer, as you know. We can go there if you want; I mean, is it something, when you look at the economic fallout in general, even if there's a truce or lasting peace, how long does that play out for? I think there are two dimensions to that. One is for shipping to resume under regular, normal conditions. It will take, I don't know whether it's two or three months; I listen to the experts. I try to understand how long it takes to refill the ships again, move the tankers around, and deliver. I think most of them are saying that it's a two or three-month job for the shipping and the distribution. Production and refinery seem to be another matter. When you look at the Ra's al-Asad refinery facility in Qatar, they say it's not months; it's years that we're talking about. Some of the facilities in Saudi, whether it's pipelines, are probably shorter, but some of the other facilities will require long-term fixing, adjusting, and repairing to set them up again. Given all of this, is there too much financial exuberance in markets? You know, what is a little strange is that there is a tendency by some to assume that it's business as usual. I think there is a bit of a dichotomy between those who regard it as business as usual and those, on the other hand, who are saying, "watch out, it's a very significant shock, and we are not about to see the end of that process." There will be ramifications; there will be an impact on the price of processed and unprocessed food. There will be an impact on the supply chain if it lasts longer. We're not going to see exactly the same disruption as during COVID, but something that will be in between normal and COVID disruption. But do you worry about financial stability? I mean, there's also private markets. I always do. I always worry about financial stability and price stability because they are intrinsically interdependent. In the supervision function of the ECB and all supervisors around the world, we have to be very attentive to financial risks—whether they are declared and disclosed risks, which hopefully have mitigating factors, or the rampant risks and the various "black swans" that are there. What worries you the most in all of this? It's what worries me, both in good and bad—and I hope we'll never have the ugly—is the impact of artificial intelligence on our economies. It is the outcome and the governance of artificial intelligence. What I mean by that is: what impact will it have on productivity where our star will go, which obviously matters to us central bankers? But what impact will it have on our societies? How many people will be unemployed? How many people will require retraining and reskilling? Who will pay for that? The whole fiscal equilibrium will result from the massive transformation that is expected in many corners. That keeps me—not awake at night, because I try to get some sleep—but it's a big issue. It's a really big issue. Are governments thinking about that given they're also dealing with this massive energy crisis? I've been in governments a few years of my life. What is really difficult is to focus on the day-to-day, to mind the next election, and to respect your program or the expectations that your voters have. And yet, at the same time, you must anticipate what will be the effect of major breakthrough innovations and significant developments. I think that in that category, AI is something that we need to have at the front of our minds. It is moving so fast, and the impact of it can be so disruptive for good or for bad. I think the development we've seen with Anthropic and Mythos is a good example of a responsible company thinking that it could be really good, but if it falls in the wrong hands, it could be really bad. We need to do something about it. I don't think there is a framework. Everybody is keen to have a framework within which to operate, but I don't think there is a governance framework that is there to actually mind those things. We need to work on that. Do you think financial institutions are prepared for it? I think they've been called to meetings in various corners of the world, and I know that we at the ECB are talking to financial institutions to alert them to those risks, yes. Madame Lagarde, we also had elections in Hungary over the weekend. What does Orban's defeat mean for Europe and the world? I think I'm looking at Peter Magyar's victory, which was a significant landslide result. He has a two-thirds majority. He can change things. This new Prime Minister can change the constitution and remove a number of obstacles to both growth and integrity in the governance of that country. I think that is really to be welcomed. The second thing that it tells me is that Hungary is in the European family. What I heard recently is that this new Prime Minister, Peter Magyar, is indicating that he would like to see Hungary join the Euro area. He wants the Forint of Hungary to be replaced by the Euro. I think this is the natural path for all 27—well, 26 actually, as it excludes Denmark. But 26 member states are expected to be part of the family. We have 21 currently. If Hungary goes through the process of integration and convergence, it would be a great achievement. Do you think that the talks on the adoption of the Euro by Hungary will happen in your mandate? Wow. You'd have to extend my mandate a little bit, which I think is not in the cards. I'm happy to stay, but no, because convergence requires a number of reforms. I think Hungary is going to consider and hopefully implement those; the judicial one is a case in point. Then it requires an adjustment period, which is usually anywhere between two and a few more years, depending on which country you look at. The convergence process takes a bit of time. You need to align a lot of parameters, and you need to have the same legal basis on many aspects. But I'm delighted that he's looking at it with a very positive approach. He is speaking up on that basis, which has not been the case for a long time. Talking about your mandate, will you see it out? Will you fully finish your mandate? Look, when there are big clouds on the horizon, the captain does not leave the ship. And this captain is not going to leave the ship because I see clouds. Biggest cloud? Well, you know them. But what do you see? We're all facing the same situation with asymmetry, as I indicated. But when you see major disruption and the energy supply being reduced, you see threats to growth and upside risk to inflation. These are serious matters that we have to be attentive to and keep under control. So our price stability, our two percent target, is not going to be varied from. Madame Lagarde, Captain Lagarde, thank you so much for joining us. Thank you. Matt, in the latter part of that, mentioned the price tag. What is the value of one of these things? Well, we never talk about money; it's rude. But just to give you an indication, if you were looking at it, it sits somewhere between our one-of-one coach-built motor cars and our very complex private commissions. I mean, I got to say this, like, probably fair to say this is the most expensive EV ever, right? At least is that the ballpark we're talking about? I wouldn't want to comment any further on that right now. We're talking about three to five million dollars probably. I would guess closer to the higher end of that range. Hannah Elliott wrote a fantastic piece on it and she shows in her story the Rolls-Royce 17 EX, which inspired the shape of the new car. So cool. Who's buying these things? Can you give us any names? Well, the wonderful thing is that the client base for this motor car was involved in the project two years ago. So they've been on the inner circle. And the great news is this is the first time that Rolls-Royce motor cars will deliver a coach-built motor car into the United States. In fact, about a third of the allocation of motor cars that we'll build will come to this country. Is this—I mean, are you moving away from being a car maker and turning into, you know, a luxury experience provider or a marketer of exclusivity? I mean, it seems like it's customization built on a fixed chassis. Well, Rolls-Royce is perfectly placed today to really take advantage of the shifting trend in luxury where it's all about the experience as well as what is actually created. And so every Rolls-Royce that we build today is for a client because they've requested it. Coach build allows us to show our potential clients the possibilities of their Rolls-Royce. So coach build collections are a very important project for us and it's a permanent part of our product offer now. What does the geographical spread of clients for this car look like? It is literally all over the world. And that's the wonderful thing. So these are motor cars that will be seen. We have clients in Asia, we have clients in Europe, we have clients in America. So of course, Project Nightingale will be seen and they will be driven. Many of the clients who I know are looking forward to taking delivery of their motor car have told me that they will use it every day. What are you learning at Rolls-Royce that you can pass on to Oliver Zipse and your parent company, BMW? I was down in your private office, for example, and I saw the level of customization that's possible. The sort of understated luxury of the place, the elegance of it really blew me away. But it strikes me that you're not just a halo brand for BMW, but you also are kind of a luxury laboratory for them. Rolls-Royce is a true house of luxury. Everything we produce is because the clients asked us to produce it. But what we've seen in the last few years is a clear growth in demand for more complex commissions. Since we've opened the private office here in New York, the level of demand for those commissions has increased dramatically. And we're seeing that in all parts of the world. How do Middle Eastern deliveries look? We know Ferrari pulled back amidst a war. How are you catering to clients in that region that are dealing with the war on their doorstep? Well, of course, the Middle East situation is one that we're watching very, very closely. It's an important region for us, and our thoughts are very much with the people that are affected by the situation right now. However, demand for Rolls-Royce globally is still very, very strong. So of course, it's too early to say what sort of impact that may have. I don't think anyone knows at this stage. But you're still able to sell a hundred five-million-dollar cars, let's just say I pick a number. Ferrari can do this as well now; you see Pagani and Koenigsegg doing this. I mean, is this an unprecedented time where the amount of people that are so wealthy to buy this many products at that level exist? It's clear that the ultra-high-net-worth audience is continuing to grow. But their tastes for luxury are very much shifting away from just having objects to having experiences or creating objects which are meaningful or personally resonant. And that really plays to Rolls-Royce's strengths. Everything we create is because a client has asked us to do it. And Project Nightingale is a great example of that where not just the motor car itself, but the client journey is something which is truly remarkable. Remember, these clients have been in the inner circle for two years. And they'll be part of the testing process for the motor car as well. So their experience is something that quite literally money can't buy. This is about a relationship that we have with our clients. One of the experiences I would love to have, and will hopefully someday soon, is a tour of your factory at Goodwood. This is the estate of the Duke of Richmond, where the Festival of Speed happens, and where you have your factory which normally puts out your production cars. But now you've invested some massive sum—I think 300 million pounds—to enable this luxury process. What was involved in that? Well, it's very clear that demand for more complex commissions is increasing and it's increasing rapidly. That's a core part of the Rolls-Royce luxury model. And so we've extended the facility in Goodwood so that we've got space for those commissions. It also increases our capacity for more complex paint finishes on motor cars and also these coach-build projects too. The whole principle of Rolls-Royce is about scarcity. So we extend our factory so that we can create more value for each motor car rather than creating more volume of motor cars. So you're—by the way, do you share your margins? What are your margins and how have they grown? We don't talk about specific details. But what I can tell you is we make a very meaningful contribution to our shareholder, BMW Group. All right, it's been great having you here, Chris Brownridge. I hope the next time I see you is at Goodwood. I hope so too. I would like to go. I don't know when the... I want to go to the Revival. So they have the Revival there, Dani. Do you have to explain to me what that is? The Festival of Speed is exactly what it sounds like, with modern cars and everything. But the Revival is like a throwback. So you dress—everyone does—as if you were in the pre-war era. Wait, sorry, what does that mean, you dress as if you were in the pre-war era? What are you wearing? It's like you go dressed as Downton Abbey basically, or whatever you like, right? Or true 1950s clothing. It's a very special event. I'd say it's one of the best events on the calendar. And there are a lot of pilots, you know; you bring your P-51 Mustang or you show up in your Rolls-Royce Nightingale and... What if you're from New York City and you don't own a car? What do you show up in? I guess you... am I still allowed? You're still very welcome. Okay, thank you. Chris Brownridge, CEO of Rolls-Royce. John Spencer on What the Headlines Get Wrong About the Iran War | The Real Eisman Playbook Ep 55

Steve Eisman(168.0K) ·64:55 ·13.5K views · 2026-04-14
Steve Eisman (Host, The Real Eisman Playbook) John Spencer (Retired U.S. Army Officer)
Despite media speculation, John Spencer asserts that the Trump administration has been "beyond consistent" regarding its four core goals:
TL;DR: The United States and Israel have achieved unprecedented military success in "Operation Epic Fury," systematically dismantling Iran’s leadership and military infrastructure through a historic decapitation strike and high-precision warfare. While the closing of the Strait...

TL;DR: The United States and Israel have achieved unprecedented military success in "Operation Epic Fury," systematically dismantling Iran’s leadership and military infrastructure through a historic decapitation strike and high-precision warfare. While the closing of the Strait of Hormuz remains a primary Iranian economic weapon, the war has demonstrated a level of U.S. military dominance that is forcing a strategic re-evaluation by global powers like China and Russia.

Current Military Status and "Operation Epic Fury"

  • The Decapitation Strike: John Spencer characterizes the opening of the war (Feb 28) as historic and unprecedented in military science.
      • Leadership Elimination: The U.S. and Israel successfully killed the Supreme Leader, the head of the IRGC, and over 40 top political and military leaders simultaneously.
      • Technological Dominance: The strike utilized deep intelligence penetration (including tapping highway cameras) and synchronized precision munitions to hit leaders even in underground bunkers during daylight.
  • Degradation of Means:
      • Naval Attrition: John Spencer estimates that 95% of the Iranian Navy has been destroyed since the conflict began.
      • Missile Capabilities: Iran's ballistic missile stockpile is estimated to be degraded by 50% to 75%. While Iran once launched volleys of 300 per day, they are now down to roughly 30.
      • Target Precision: Out of 15,000 targets struck, nearly 100% were hit with precision-guided munitions, a massive leap from the 10% precision rate seen in the First Gulf War.
  • Defense Efficiency: Approximately 90% of Iranian missile and drone launches are currently being intercepted.

The Four Official U.S. Objectives

Despite media speculation, John Spencer asserts that the Trump administration has been "beyond consistent" regarding its four core goals:

1. Destroy Missile Infrastructure: Eliminating the stockpile and manufacturing sites that protect Iranian aggression.

2. Eliminate the Navy: Removing Iran’s ability to coerce the world through naval threats.

3. Nuclear Denial: Ensuring Iran never achieves a functional nuclear weapon.

4. Neutralize Proxies: Breaking the "Ring of Fire" (Hezbollah, Hamas, Houthis) and preventing the export of terrorism.

  • Regime Change: Spencer clarifies that while desirable, regime change is *not* an official objective. The U.S. strategy focuses on changing the regime's behavior and capacity to wage war. He notes that the theocracy could theoretically survive if it capitulates to these four demands to ensure its own survival.

Economic Coercion and the Strait of Hormuz

  • The Toll Booth Strategy: Iran is using "threat-based" closure of the Strait of Hormuz rather than a physical blockade to drive up insurance costs and create global instability.
  • Impact on China: Contrary to some headlines, China is the primary victim of the Strait's closure.
      • Energy Dependence: China receives 50% of its oil from the Gulf region.
      • Strategic Miscalculation: Iran’s strategy aims to pressure the U.S. via the markets, but it is disproportionately crippling its own "gas station" (China).
  • Economic Center of Gravity: Kharg Island remains Iran’s most vulnerable asset, responsible for 90% of its oil exports and 60% of its revenue. Spencer suggests that destroying this hub would "cripple" the regime's remaining vitality.

Global Geopolitical Realignment

  • The "Prowess Show": The war serves as a massive demonstration of U.S. conventional power.
      • B-2 Bomber Utility: Spencer highlights "Operation Midnight Hammer," where B-2 bombers flew 18-hour round trips from Missouri to bomb Iran, achieving total surprise through complex deception operations.
      • Deterrence for China: Steve Eisman notes that China must now rethink an invasion of Taiwan, having seen that Russian and Chinese-made air defense systems (used by Iran) are ineffective against U.S. technology.
  • Dismantling the "Ring of Fire": Iran’s multi-billion dollar investment in regional proxies has been largely neutralized. Hezbollah is "de-fanged," and Hamas is "basically destroyed."
  • The Special Forces Rescue: The successful recovery of a U.S. Colonel ("Wizzo") from deep within Iranian territory, without a single loss of life, is cited as one of the most complex and successful rescue operations in military history.

Notable Quotes

John Spencer: "What if you could have killed Hitler, his successor, all of his field marshals, and all the generals... all at once? None of the military theorists even imagined being able to take out an entire leadership... it has never happened."
John Spencer: "The biggest person who is hurting from the Strait of Hormuz being closed right now is China... China gets 50% of its oil from the Gulf. It's the gas station for China. That entire plan has been unstructured."
Steve Eisman: "The US has put on basically a show—an incredible military prowess show that has never been seen before. And Russia and China are taking notes... President Xi of China has got to be rethinking, at least on some level, invading Taiwan."
John Spencer: "The United States has demonstrated a capability that no other country in the world has. And that should put fear in other great powers if they want to go toe-to-toe."
